\\textcolor[rgb]{0.7,0.1,0.1}{t}%\n%         \\textcolor[rgb]{0.8,0.4,0.1}{h}%\n%         \\textcolor[rgb]{0.7,0.7,0.0}{u}%\n%         \\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.5,0.1}{b}%\n%         \\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.5,0.5}{.}%\n%         \\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.1,0.7}{i}%\n%         \\textcolor[rgb]{0.5,0.1,0.5}{o}%\n%         }}\n%     \\end{center}\n% \\end{figure*}\n\n\\begin{abstract}\n% Reasoning...Self-evolving training...However, there still remains some significant questions that how to design effective self-evolving training framework and what is the \n% Self-evolving training, where models are trained on their own outputs, has been a primary method for complex tasks like reasoning, since high-quality chain-of-thought data are hard to acquire for these tasks. However, as a more complicated and difficult task, multimodal reasoning, the effect of self-evolving training for it is hardly explored. Also, self-evolving training involves many different factors, the understanding for self-eovlving training reamins limited. Furthermore, self-evolving training is easy to meet the saturation issue, which hinders the further improvement and scalability for these methods. In this paper, we aim to explore self-evolving training in more general scenario and answer these questions that hinder the self-evolving training. \n\n% Self-evolving training, where models learn from their own outputs, has become a primary approach for complex tasks like reasoning, given the scarcity of high-quality chain-of-thought data. However, its effectiveness in multimodal reasoning, a more challenging and intricate task beyond text-only reasoning, remains largely unexplored. Additionally, self-evolving training involves multiple factors, yet our understanding of it remains limited. Furthermore, one critical issue for this training method is saturation, which restricts further improvement and scalability. To address these issues, we draw inspiration from reinforcement learning (RL) and investigate the intricacies of self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning through the lens of RL, pinpointing three key factors: \\emph{Training Method}, \\emph{Reward Model}, and \\emph{Prompt Variation}. We systematically analyze each factor and explore a set of best designs for training which sucessfully improves the ability of models for multimodal reasoning by a large margin. Furthermore, we dive deeper into the training dynamics to investigate the saturation of self-evolving training and propose a new automatic balancing mechanism to alleviate this issue. After all the investigations, we present our final framework for \\mevol\\ (\\textbf{M}ultimodal \\textbf{S}elf-evolving \\textbf{T}r\\textbf{a}ining for \\textbf{R}easoning), which is universally effective for models with different sizes on various benchmarks. All resources will be released to help fully understand and improve self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning.\n\nSelf-evolving training‚Äîwhere models iteratively learn from their own outputs‚Äîhas emerged as a key approach for complex reasoning tasks, addressing the scarcity of high-quality chain-of-thought data. However, its effectiveness in multimodal reasoning, a domain more intricate than text-only reasoning, remains underexplored, and the understanding of critical factors in this training paradigm remains limited. Furthermore, a central challenge for this training method is performance saturation, which impedes further improvements and scalability. Inspired by reinforcement learning (RL), in this paper, we reframe self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning through the lens of RL, identifying three pivotal factors: \\emph{Training Method}, \\emph{Reward Model}, and \\emph{Prompt Variation}. Through systematic analysis, we establish relatively optimal design principles that significantly enhance multimodal reasoning capabilities. Moreover, delving deeper into training dynamics, we uncover the roots of saturation and propose a new automatic balancing mechanism to mitigate this limitation. Building on these insights, we propose \\mevol\\ (\\textbf{M}ultimodal \\textbf{S}elf-evolving \\textbf{T}raining for \\textbf{R}easoning), a framework that achieves consistent performance gains across models of varying sizes and diverse benchmarks. All resources are made publicly available at \\href{https://mstar-lmm.github.io}{\\texttt{%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.7,0.1,0.1}{h}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.8,0.4,0.1}{t}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.7,0.7,0.0}{t}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.5,0.1}{p}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.5,0.5}{s}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.1,0.7}{:}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.5,0.1,0.5}{/}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.5,0.1,0.5}{/}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.7,0.1,0.1}{m}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.8,0.4,0.1}{s}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.7,0.7,0.0}{t}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.5,0.1}{a}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.5,0.5}{r}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.1,0.7}{-}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.5,0.1,0.5}{l}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.7,0.1,0.1}{m}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.8,0.4,0.1}{m}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.5,0.5}{.}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.1,0.7}{g}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.5,0.1,0.5}{i}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.7,0.1,0.1}{t}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.8,0.4,0.1}{h}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.7,0.7,0.0}{u}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.5,0.1}{b}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.5,0.5}{.}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.1,0.1,0.7}{i}%\n\\textcolor[rgb]{0.5,0.1,0.5}{o}%\n}.}\n\n% Self-evolving training, where models iteratively learn from their own outputs, has emerged as a key approach for complex reasoning tasks, particularly given the scarcity of high-quality chain-of-thought data. However, its effectiveness in multimodal reasoning‚Äîwhich extends beyond text-only reasoning‚Äîremains largely unexplored. Moreover, self-evolving training involves multiple interdependent factors, yet our understanding of its underlying mechanisms is still limited. A critical challenge in this paradigm is saturation, which hinders further improvement and scalability.\n% To address these issues, we draw inspiration from reinforcement learning (RL) and systematically investigate the intricacies of self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning through three key factors: Training Method, Reward Model, and Prompt Variation. We analyze each factor in depth and propose an optimized training strategy that significantly enhances multimodal reasoning capabilities. Additionally, we delve into the training dynamics to examine the saturation issue and introduce a novel automatic balancing mechanism to mitigate it.\n% Based on these insights, we present \\mevol\\ (\\textbf{M}ultimodal \\textbf{S}elf-evolving \\textbf{T}r\\textbf{a}ining for \\textbf{R}easoning)‚Äîa universally effective framework that consistently improves performance across models of varying sizes and multiple benchmarks. All resources will be released to facilitate further research and advancement in self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning.\n\n\n\n% \\footnote{We believe this study fills a significant gap in the understanding of self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning and offers a robust framework for future research. Our policy and reward models, as well as the collected data, will be released to facilitate further investigation in multimodal reasoning.} \n\n% for self-evolving training in multimodal reasoning, encapsulating these design choices into a framework we call\n% \\mevol\\ (\\textbf{M}ultimodal \\textbf{S}elf-evolving \\textbf{T}r\\textbf{a}ining for \\textbf{R}easoning), \n% which is universally effective for models with different sizes on various benchmarks,\n% e.g., surpassing the pre-evolved model significantly on 5 multimodal reasoning benchmarks without using additional human annotations, as demonstrated on MiniCPM-V-2.5 (8B), Phi-3.5-Vision (4B) and InternVL2 (2B).\n% We believe this study fills a significant gap in the understanding of self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning and offers a robust framework for future research. Our policy and reward models, as well as the collected data, will be released to facilitate further investigation in multimodal reasoning.\n\n% In this paper, we delve into the intricacies of self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning via the lens of reinforcement learning \n\n% we systematically investigate self-evolving training in a broader context and address key challenges that hinder its progress.\n\n% While multimodal reasoning has not been explored... The understanding, intricacies...Furthermore, saturation... has been emerged as a primary method... To bridge this gap, fully explore self-evolving training method, we ...\n% Reasoning ability is essential for Large Multimodal Models (LMMs). \n\n% In the absence of multimodal chain-of-thought annotated data, self-evolving training, where the model learns from its own outputs, has emerged as an effective and scalable approach for enhancing reasoning abilities. \n% Despite its growing usage, a comprehensive understanding of self-evolving training, particularly in the context of multimodal reasoning, remains limited. In this paper, we delve into the intricacies of self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning, pinpointing three key factors: \\emph{Training Method}, \\emph{Reward Model}, and \\emph{Prompt Variation}. We systematically examine each factor and explore how various configurations affect the training's effectiveness. Our analysis leads to a set of best practices for each factor, aimed at optimizing multimodal reasoning.\n% Furthermore, we explore the \\emph{Self-Evolution Dynamics} during training and the impact of automatic balancing mechanisms in boosting performance. After all the investigations, we present a final recipe for self-evolving training in multimodal reasoning, encapsulating these design choices into a framework we call\n% \\mevol\\ (\\textbf{M}ultimodal \\textbf{S}elf-evolving \\textbf{T}r\\textbf{a}ining for \\textbf{R}easoning), \n% which is universally effective for models with different sizes on various benchmarks,\n% e.g., surpassing the pre-evolved model significantly on 5 multimodal reasoning benchmarks without using additional human annotations, as demonstrated on MiniCPM-V-2.5 (8B), Phi-3.5-Vision (4B) and InternVL2 (2B).\n% We believe this study fills a significant gap in the understanding of self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning and offers a robust framework for future research. Our policy and reward models, as well as the collected data, will be released to facilitate further investigation in multimodal reasoning.\n\\end{abstract}\n\n\\section{Introduction}\n\n% \\wl{New intro} \n% With the rapid advancement of Large Language Models (LLMs), their reasoning capabilities have seen remarkable progress \\citep{shao2024deepseekmath, xin2024deepseekproverv15harnessingproofassistant, yang2024qwen25mathtechnicalreportmathematical}. However, the scarcity of high-quality, human-annotated chain-of-thought data poses a critical challenge for advancing complex reasoning, particularly in multimodal scenarios where textual and visual reasoning intersect. \nMultimodal reasoning is a fundamental skill in many real-world applications, such as intelligent agents \\citep{liu2024visualagentbenchlargemultimodalmodels}, robotics \\citep{li2023manipllmembodiedmultimodallarge, liu2024selfcorrectedmultimodallargelanguage}, and autonomous driving \\citep{yang2023lidarllmexploringpotentiallarge}. It requires Large Multimodal Models (LMMs) to understand various modalities beyond text. For example, visual mathematical reasoning~\\citep{lu2023mathvista} challenges models to analyze complex figures, diagrams, and charts, leveraging the provided\ninformation to perform reasoning tasks.\n\nDespite the critical role of mutlimodal reasoning, the availability of human-annotated thought processes in multimodal scenarios remains limited, hindering the learning of multimodal reasoning. \n% To further improve the reasoning abilities for models, \nConsequently, self-evolving training, which utilizes model's own generation ability to iteratively tune and improve itself without external annotated data, has emerged as an appealing candidate to facilitate reasoning abilities.\nWhile research on self-evolving training has primarily focused on the text-only settings \\citep{hosseini2024vstar,sun2024easy2hard,shao2024deepseekmath}, its application in the multimodal domain, especially for reasoning tasks, has been limited with only a few sporadic examples \\citep{fang2024vila2,dubey2024llama3, deng2024enhancing}, and a unified framework has yet to be established.\n% Self-evolving training, a paradigm where models iteratively learn from their own outputs, has emerged as a promising solution for text-only reasoning tasks \\citep{hosseini2024vstar,sun2024easy2hard}. Yet, its effectiveness for multimodal reasoning remains underexplored, with only sporadic efforts \\citep{fang2024vila2,dubey2024llama3, deng2024enhancing}, and understanding of critical components within this self-evolving training paradigm remains limited. Furthermore, such paradigms face a key challenge: performance saturation, where models stagnate despite continued training, hindering scalability and limiting further advancements.\n\n\n% Self-evolving training‚Äîwhere models iteratively learn from their own outputs‚Äîhas emerged as a key approach for complex reasoning tasks, addressing the scarcity of high-quality chain-of-thought data. However, its effectiveness in multimodal reasoning, a domain more intricate than text-only reasoning, remains underexplored, and the understanding of critical factors in this training paradigm remain limited. Furthermore, a central challenge for this training method is performance saturation, which impedes further improvements and scalability. Inspired by reinforcement learning (RL), in this paper, we reframe self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning through an RL lens, identifying three pivotal factors: \\emph{Training Method}, \\emph{Reward Model}, and \\emph{Prompt Variation}. Through systematic analysis, we establish an optimal design principles that significantly enhance multimodal reasoning capabilities. Moreover, delving deeper into training dynamics, we uncover the roots of saturation and propose a new automatic balancing mechanism to mitigate this limitation. Building on these insights, we propose \\mevol\\ (\\textbf{M}ultimodal \\textbf{S}elf-evolving \\textbf{T}raining for \\textbf{R}easoning), a framework that achieves consistent performance gains across models of varying sizes and diverse benchmarks. All resources will be made publicly available.With the rapid advancement of Large Language Models, their reasoning abilities have improved significantly \\citep{shao2024deepseekmath, xin2024deepseekproverv15harnessingproofassistant, yang2024qwen25mathtechnicalreportmathematical}. This progress has been accompanied by a growing demand for more realistic and general reasoning capabilities.\n% % , including the ability to handle complex and general reasoning tasks. \n% Multimodal reasoning, considered a fundamental skill in many real-world applications, such as intelligent agents \\citep{liu2024visualagentbenchlargemultimodalmodels}, robotics \\citep{li2023manipllmembodiedmultimodallarge, liu2024selfcorrectedmultimodallargelanguage}, and autonomous driving \\citep{yang2023lidarllmexploringpotentiallarge}, exemplifies this trend. \n% % As a more practical application, \n% Multimodal reasoning requires Large Multimodal Models (LMMs) to understand various modalities beyond text. For example, visual mathematical reasoning \\citep{lu2023mathvista} challenges models to analyze complex figures, diagrams, and charts, leveraging the provided information to perform reasoning tasks.\n\n% Despite these advances, the availability of human-annotated thought processes in multimodal scenarios remains limited, challenging the learning of multimodal reasoning. \n% % To further improve the reasoning abilities for models, \n% Consequently, self-evolving training, which utilizes model's own generation ability to iteratively tune and improve itself without external annotated data, has emerged as an appealing candidate to facilitate reasoning abilities.\n% % a widely-adopted way for its effectiveness and scalability \\citep{huang2022llmcanselfimprove, zelikman2022star, gulcehre2023rest,singh2023restem}. \n% While research on self-evolving training has primarily focused on the text-only settings \\citep{hosseini2024vstar,sun2024easy2hard,shao2024deepseekmath}, its application in the multimodal domain, especially for reasoning tasks, has been limited with only a few sporadic examples \\citep{fang2024vila2,dubey2024llama3, deng2024enhancing}, and a unified\n% % \\fan{maybe replace it with `comprehensive', means we dive deep and perform thorough study of this direction}\n% framework has yet to be established.\n% % \\jh{Fang et al. does not apply for reasoning right? it is mainly about normal QA and caption, not sure about llama3 though.}\n% % \\jh{I don't understand why we call it ``unified''}\n\n% % In this work, we choose to investigate Online Reject Tuning \\citep{yuan2023rft}, which is a more scalable and stable implementation of self-evolving methods compared with RL algorithms like PPO \\citep{schulman2017proximal}. \n% % \\fan{some starting word: e.g.,  `Motivated by the need to fill this gap', we xxxxx and xxxx. we then identify xxx componenets: xxx} \n% Inspired by the fact that self-evolving training can actually be formulated as a general reinforcement learning (RL) framework (\\S~\\ref{sec:overview}), we first identify the three key components of self-evolving training in multimodal reasoning via the lens of RL, namely the \\textbf{training method}, the use of \\textbf{reward model} and the \\textbf{prompt variation}, to build a clear and unified design space for searching the optimal strategies. Through massive controlled experiments, \n% % \\jh{plz add the section references for each of the points}\n% we (1) propose a continuous self-evolving training scheme that inherits optimizers states from the last iteration to reduce the gap towards full online learning and outperforms other iterative baselines (\\S \\ref{sec:training_methods}); (2) train the first multimodal, process-based reward model for multimodal reasoning and demonstrate its usefulness in further enhancing performance (\\S \\ref{sec:rm}); and (3) find that adding more unlabeled queries helps only when having perfect reward signals (e.g., the oracle groundtruth answers), and it hurts the performance if the reward model does not generalize well on unseen data (\\S \\ref{sec:unlabeled}).\n\n\\begin{figure*}[!t]\n    \\centering\n    \\includegraphics[width=0.9\\linewidth]{figures/main_figure_v4_color.pdf}\n    \\caption{Overview of our self-evolving training framework for multimodal reasoning. We investigate the three essential design components of it, namely \\textcolor{highlightedinfig1}{Training method ($\\mathcal{T}$)}, \\textcolor{highlightedinfig1}{Reward model ($\\mathcal{R}$)}, and \\textcolor{highlightedinfig1}{Prompt variation ($\\mathcal{P}$)}. Orthogonal to the static factors, the \\textcolor{highlightedinfig1}{Dynamics of self-evoloution} is also monitered, and provides control signals to the training process.\n    % \\fan{(1)red color in caption always reminds me of un-solved comments; maybe just black color with bold font.(2) add \\mevol name, not just self-evolving?}\n    }\n    \\label{fig:overview}\n    \\vspace{-10pt}\n\\end{figure*}\n\n\nInspired by reinforcement learning (RL), in this paper, we reframe self-evolving training through the lens of RL, identifying three factors that are critical inside self-evolving training: the \\textbf{training method}, the use of \\textbf{reward model}, and \\textbf{prompt variation}. Through massive controlled studies, we (1) propose a continuous self-evolving training scheme \n% that inherits optimizers states from the last iteration \nto reduce the gap towards full online learning and outperforms other iterative baselines (\\S \\ref{sec:training_methods}); (2) train the first multimodal, process-based reward model for multimodal reasoning and demonstrate its usefulness in further enhancing performance (\\S \\ref{sec:rm}); and (3) find that adding more unlabeled queries helps only when having perfect reward signals (e.g., the oracle groundtruth answers), and it hurts the performance if the reward model does not generalize well on unseen data (\\S \\ref{sec:unlabeled}). Beyond static design principles, we investigate the training dynamics, revealing how performance saturation stems from diminishing exploration potential during training. To address this, we introduce a new metric that bridges exploration and exploitation, and propose an automatic balancing mechanism that dynamically adjusts the sampling temperature to sustain exploration-exploitation trade-offs.\n\nCombining all the recipes concluded through separate, controlled studies, we propose our self-evolving training algorithm named as \\mevol~(\\textbf{M}ultimodal \\textbf{S}elf-evolving \\textbf{T}r\\textbf{a}ining for \\textbf{R}easoning).\nOur experimental results on 5 different multimodal reasoning benchmarks, including MathVista, M3CoT, MMStar, MMBench and AI2D, show that this strategy, which incorporates both optimized static design choices and dynamic adjustments, effectively mitigates exploration loss during training and enhances performance universally for models with varied sizes such as MiniCPM-V-2.5 (8B), Phi-3.5-Vision (4B) and InternVL2 (2B).\n\n% Orthogonal to the static factors above, we also dive into the dynamic evolving process of the model, namely the \\textbf{dynamics of self-evolution} to see how model's behavior changes during the training.\n% We find that although the model performance of greedy decoding increases, its exploration potential keeps decreasing through the training (\\S \\ref{sec:monitor}), then we introduce a mechanism to dynamically adjust the sampling temperature during training to balance exploration and exploitation (\\S \\ref{sec:final_recipe}), as shown in Figure \\ref{fig:overview}.\n\n% Combining all the recipes concluded through separate, controlled studies, we propose our self-evolving training algorithm named as \\mevol~(\\textbf{M}ultimodal \\textbf{S}elf-evolving \\textbf{T}r\\textbf{a}ining for \\textbf{R}easoning). \n% Our experimental results on 5 different multimodal reasoning benchmarks, including MathVista, M3CoT, MMStar, MMBench and AI2D, show that this strategy, which incorporates both optimized static design choices and dynamic adjustments, effectively mitigates exploration loss during training and further enhances performance universally for models with different sizes such as MiniCPM-V-2.5 (8B), Phi-3.5-Vision (4B) and InternVL2 (2B).\n\n\n\\section{Overview of Self-Evolving Training for Multimodal Reasoning}\n\\label{sec:overview}\n% This section serves as Preliminary for self-evolving training, we identify the three components here\n\n% \\begin{itemize}\n%     \\item It is just a draft instead of the final framework \\\\\n%     \\item Self-Evolve, On-policy, SFT insufficient, cause hallucination, therefore self-evolve as a more online(on-policy) method is better \\wl{cite some of \\citep{guo2024direct,kumar2024traininglanguagemodelsselfcorrect,setlur2024rleightfold,gekhman2024doesfinetuningllmsnew}} \\\\\n%     \\item Markov Decision Process \\\\\n% \\end{itemize}\n\n% self-evolving training is a paradigm that leverages data generated by models themselves to continuously enhance their capabilities. In contrast to traditional supervised fine-tuning (SFT), which depends on human-curated data, recent studies have shown that this imitation learning approach can be less efficient \\citep{guo2024direct} and may heighten the risk of hallucinations \\citep{gekhman2024doesfinetuningllmsnew} compared to other methods like rejective fine-tuning (RFT) \\citep{yuan2023rft} or Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) \\citep{schulman2017proximal}, which rely more on on-policy data. Particularly for complex reasoning tasks, training methods that utilize on-policy data have been shown to more effectively enhance models' reasoning abilities \\citep{yuan2023rft,tong2024dartmath,metallama3}.\n\nSelf-evolving training can be modeled as a general framework of reinforcement learning, where various algorithms can be formulated as a specific instantiation of RL, such as PPO \\citep{schulman2017proximal}, STaR~\\citep{zelikman2022star}, ReST~\\citep{gulcehre2023rest} and ReST$^{EM}$~\\citep{singh2023restem}.\n% Many of these approaches are essentially iterative SFT training (e.g., ReST, ReST$^{EM}$, STaR) with slight variations, \nSpecifically, given a reward function $\\mathcal{R}$, the objective of self-evolving training\n% \\jh{I have modified several places, we should use self-evolving consistently} \nis to train the policy model $\\pi_\\theta$ to maximize expectation of reward $\\mathcal{R}$:\n\\begin{equation}\n    \\pi_{\\theta} = \\operatorname{argmax}_{\\pi_{\\theta}}\\sum_i^L \\mathbb{E}_{x,o \\sim \\mathcal{D},\\hat{y}_i \\sim \\pi_{\\theta}[\\cdot|x,o]}[\\mathcal{R}(\\hat{y}_i)],\n\\end{equation}\nwhere $x$, $o$ represent the query and image in the given training data $\\mathcal{D}$, while $\\hat{y}_i$ is a response sampled from the current policy model $\\pi_\\theta$.\nThis standard RL objective, however, can be unstable to optimize and difficult to scale up, thus a popular algorithm adopted by recent works is to decouple the response rollout $\\hat{y}_i \\sim \\pi_{\\theta}[\\cdot|x,o]$ and policy improvement into separate offline stages~\\citep{gulcehre2023rest,singh2023restem}: (1) \\emph{Generate}: the current policy model generates new responses $\\hat{y}_i \\sim \\pi_{\\theta}[\\cdot|x,o]$; and (2) \\emph{Improve}: using the rewards to selects certain responses from the Generate step, which are then used to train the policy model with a standard supervised fine-tuning (SFT) loss. This way, the algorithm resembles Rejection Fine-Tuning (RFT,~\\citet{yuan2023rft}) as it filters out negative responses in a hard manner. \nBoth steps are performed iteratively to strike a tradeoff between offline and online training. In many tasks such as mathematical problem-solving, there exists a unique, ground-truth answer $a^*$ which is utilized in the reward function, for example,~\\citet{singh2023restem} directly adopts exact match to compute a binary reward by comparing $\\hat{y}$ and $a^*$.\nIn such an iterative training procedure, the objective at iteration $t$ is to obtain an improved policy model $\\pi^{t+1}_\\theta$:\n% to maximize expectation of reward $\\mathcal{R}$, starting from the current policy model $\\pi^{t}_\\theta$ and leveraging data generated by the model itself, \n% using a learning algorithm $\\mathcal{A}$. This process can be formulated as:\n% \\vspace{-1.2cm}\n\\begin{equation}\\label{eq:self_evolve}\n    \\pi_{\\theta}^{t+1} = \\operatorname{argmax}_{\\pi_{\\theta}^{t}}\\sum_i^L \\mathbb{E}_{x,o,a^* \\sim \\mathcal{D},\\hat{y}_i \\sim \\pi_{\\theta}^{t}[\\cdot|x,o]}[\\mathcal{R}(a^*,\\hat{y}_i)],\n\\end{equation}\nwhere the ground-truth answer $a^*$ can be empty, for example, when dealing with unlabeled inputs, the reward model must be able to score $\\hat{y}_i$ independently.\n\n\\textbf{The Design Spaces}\nThrough the lens of Eq.~\\ref{eq:self_evolve}, we can identify three dominant factors that influence the training process, (1) \\emph{training method}: the training algorithms to perform this iterative process vary as well.\nFor example, while~\\citet{gulcehre2023rest,xu2024chatglm} initialize the model from the last checkpoint at each iteration,~\\citet{zelikman2022star,singh2023restem}\n% \\jh{plz confirm whether star also initializes from the beginning, I vaguely remember it is} \nargue that initializing from the beginning checkpoint reduces overfitting and gives better performance empirically. (2) \\emph{reward model}: the design of reward function $\\mathcal{R}$. (3) \\emph{prompt variation}: whether to incorporate additional unlabeled inputs without $a^*$ into training. \n% \\jhc{Moreover, the iteration interval may matter as well -- although the common practice is to process the entire dataset at every iteration and the performance saturates after a few iterations, it may be suboptimal and a more online fashion with frequent iteration switch could potentially lead to improvements.}{} \\jhc{Theoretically, a short iteration interval with inherited optimizer and learning rate scheduler from the last iteration will turn this iterative optimization into a standard online RL learning algorithm.}{}\\jh{I save this to later we can introduce this in a more novel way} \nNext, we investigate these three design spaces, aiming to summarize the best practices for each factor.\n% There are different design choices to model and implement Eq.~\\ref{eq:self_evolve}, for example, the design of reward function $\\mathcal{R}$ and whether to incorporate additional unlabeled inputs without $a^*$ into training. \n% Additionally, the training algorithms to perform this iterative process vary as well.\n% For example, while~\\citet{gulcehre2023rest,xu2024chatglm} initialize the model from the last checkpoint at each iteration,~\\citet{zelikman2022star,singh2023restem}\n% % \\jh{plz confirm whether star also initializes from the beginning, I vaguely remember it is} \n% argue that initializing from the beginning checkpoint reduces overfitting and gives better performance empirically.\n% % \\jhc{Moreover, the iteration interval may matter as well -- although the common practice is to process the entire dataset at every iteration and the performance saturates after a few iterations, it may be suboptimal and a more online fashion with frequent iteration switch could potentially lead to improvements.}{} \\jhc{Theoretically, a short iteration interval with inherited optimizer and learning rate scheduler from the last iteration will turn this iterative optimization into a standard online RL learning algorithm.}{}\\jh{I save this to later we can introduce this in a more novel way} \n% Next, we investigate these three design spaces, \\emph{training method, reward model}, and \\emph{prompt variation}, aiming to summarize the best practices for each factor.\n% to faciliate multimodal reasoning learning.\n\n\n% \\begin{equation}\\label{eq:self_evolve}\n%     \\pi_{\\theta}^{t+1} = \\argmax_{\\pi_{\\theta}^{t}}{\\scriptscriptstyle \\mathcal{A}}\\sum_i^L \\mathbb{E}_{x,o,y^* \\sim \\mathcal{D},\\hat{y}_i \\sim \\pi_{\\theta}^{t}[\\cdot|x,o]}[\\mathcal{R}(y^*,\\hat{y}_i)]\n% \\end{equation}\n% \\begin{equation}\\label{eq:self_evolve}\n%     \\pi_{\\theta}^{t+1} = \\texttt{optimize}(\\pi_{\\theta}^{t},\\sum_i^L \\mathrm{E}_{x,o,y^* \\sim \\mathcal{D},\\hat{y}_i \\sim \\pi_{\\theta}^{t}[\\cdot|x,o]}[\\mathcal{R}(y^*,\\hat{y}_i)],\\mathcal{A})\n% \\end{equation}\n\n% In this context, $x$, $o$, and $y^*$ represent the prompt, image, and ground-truth response in the given training data $\\mathcal{D}$, while $\\hat{y}_i$ is a response sampled from the current policy model $\\pi^{t}_\\theta$. Equation \\ref{eq:self_evolve} closely resembles a reinforcement learning (RL) objective, and the learning algorithm can involve methods such as rejective sampling (i.e., Rejective Fine-Tuning, RFT) and Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO). Given the need for efficiency and scalability in training models for multimodal reasoning, i.e., the lengthened input sequence brought by images, this work primarily focuses on RFT-like training methods, whose effectiveness and scalability have been well-documented in previous studies \\citep{yu2023metamath, yuan2023rft, tong2024dartmath, metallama3}.\n\n\n\n% In self-evolving training \\wl{or standard RFT?}, the design of reward functions can be adapted based on the specific training objective. For multimodal reasoning tasks, the reward function is defined as $\\mathcal{R}(y^*, \\hat{y}_i) =\\mathds{1}(y^* = \\hat{y}_i)$. Then the goal of Equation \\ref{eq:self_evolve} is to find an optimal policy model that maximizes the probability of generating correct sampled responses.\n\n% Although self-evolving training has been widely adopted and shown to be highly effective, it remains unclear what makes it work and how to design a good recipe for self-training, especially in complex scenarios like multimodal reasoning. From the perspective of \\ref{eq:self_evolve}, we identify three key components inside it, including training methods (i.e. learning algorithm) $\\mathcal{A}$, reward function $\\mathcal{R}$, and policy training prompts $\\mathcal{D}=\\{(x_j, o_j, y^*_j)\\}_{j=1}^{M}$.\n\n\n% for training methods\n\n\n% for reward models\n\n% for training prompts\n% Identify the three factors.\n\n% There are different methods to design rewards... For standard RFT, reward as binary reward xxx. ORM xxx. PRM xxx.\n\n\n\n\n\n\\section{Diving into Self-Evolving Design Components}\n\\label{sec:design}\n\nIn this section, we explore the three key components of self-evolving training, examining various strategies within each. We begin by outlining the general setup (\\S \\ref{sec:setup}), followed by a comprehensive analysis of each component to identify the best practices for multimodal self-evolution (\\S \\ref{sec:training_methods}-\\S \\ref{sec:unlabeled}).\n\n\\subsection{General Setup}\n\\label{sec:setup}\n\n\\textbf{Models}\nWe base our main exploration on MiniCPM-V-2.5 (8B) \\citep{yao2024minicpmv}, and we also validate the final design choice for each component on two extra models with different sizes: Phi-3.5-Vision (4B) \\citep{abdin2024phi} and InternVL-2 (2B) \\citep{chen2024internvl}. The details of these models can be found in Appendix \\ref{app:model_details}. To make the analysis process easier to understand, we mainly present the results of MiniCPM-V-2.5 in this section, while we include the results of the other models in \\S \\ref{sec:final_recipe}. \n% For ease of space, we mainly report the MiniCPM-V-2.5 results in the following analysis with full details,  of Phi-3.5-Vision and InternVL2-2B later in \\S \\ref{sec:final_recipe}.\n\n% \\jh{I have checked Table 5, actually the results of the other two models align with miniCPM-v-2.5, right? Then we can just say our exploration is on 3 models, it is just we only report minicpm in this section, but we include the full analysis results of the other two models later in section xxx. This way, I think we can introduce all three models somewhere in the appendix. Basically we don't need to make minicpm very special in the paper}MiniCPM-V-2.5 \\citep{yao2024minicpmv}, a powerful, openly released LMM. MiniCPM-V-2.5 leverages LLaMA-3-8B \\citep{metallama3} for its language model and SigLIP \\citep{siglip} as its vision encoder, resulting in strong multimodal capabilities. Its performance on a wide range of multimodal benchmarks significantly surpasses previous openly released LMMs such as LLaVA \\citep{liu2023llava,liu2024improvedllava} and Qwen-VL \\citep{Qwen-VL}. This superior performance makes MiniCPM-V-2.5 an ideal model for investigating self-evolving training in multimodal reasoning, with fewer risks of being constrained by the model's inherent capacities.\\jh{in this version, here we need to describe that although our controlled experiments are performed only using one model due to limited computing resources, in section xxx we demonstrate that our final recipe works well for multiple different vLMs on diverse benchmarks}\n% \\jh{How minicpm-v was trained is not that important to write here, instead what matters to be written here is (1) the size of this model; (2) use 1 or 2 sentences to express minicpm-v-2.5 has strong multimodal abilities, for example, outperform qwen-vl, llava, xxx (which are basically the main reason we chose it)}\n% \\jhc{Note that toconduct controlled experiments without the confounder of model,}\n\n\n\n\\textbf{Datasets}\n% \\jh{this section 3.1 is generally too long,  I think the Datasets and the Training and Evaluation paragraphs can be merged to be shorter. Some details can be moved to appendix}\nWe utilize MathV360K \\citep{shi2024mathllava}, a high-quality and diverse multimodal reasoning dataset as our seed training dataset.\n% that includes 40K human-curated examples and 320K synthetic samples generated by GPT-4V as our seed training dataset. The images and queries in this dataset span various subjects in multimodal reasoning including algebra, arithmetic, geometry, logic, numeric commonsense and science.\nSpecifically, we downsample half of the examples (180K) from it to serve as our labeled training set, while setting aside the remaining half as a unlabeled training set by not using the answers in it. \n% For the labeled set, each data sample is composed of $(x, o, a)$ without the intermediate thought process annotation, while for the unlabeled set we only have access to $(x, o)$. \n% This is a realistic setting as there are many multimodal SFT datasets with the final answer labels, but annotated thought processes are scarce. \nFor evaluation, we split 750 samples from the unlabeled part of MathV360K as the in-domain (ID) testset.\n% , using regular expression to extract and match the answers. \nFor our out-of-domain (OOD) testset we use the \\texttt{testmini} split of MathVista \\citep{lu2023mathvista}, a comprehensive benchmark encompassing a wide range of multimodal reasoning tasks, including visual question answering, figure-based question answering, science question answering, and more. \n% We follow its original setting and use GPT-4 to extract and match the answers. \nWe also keep an non-overlapping 250 samples from MathV360K as the global validation set in training.\n% This is to mimic the setting where we have \n% The use of the remaining data will be further discussed in \n% Section \\ref{sec:unlabeled}.\n\n% \\paragraph{Warm-Up Phase to Unlock the Chain-of-Thought (CoT) Capability of LMMs}\n\n% For the prompt during the warm-up phase, please refer to Appendix \\ref{app:prompt} for more details.\n\n% While diverse responses with various intermedia steps are crucial for self-evolving training, we \n\n\n\\textbf{Main Training Settings}\nBefore self-evolving training, a self-warmup stage (Appendix \\ref{app:prompt}) is applied to enhance the CoT generation ability of all models as many LLMs tend to output final answers directly without CoT. We adopt most of the training settings from \\citet{yao2024minicpmv} (see Appendix~\\ref{app:hyperparam}), using a constant learning rate of $1e-6$ and training for 10K steps across all experiments. During all rollout phases in training, we sample 16 responses per query and set the sampling temperature to 1.0. Unless explicitly stated otherwise, we follow existing practices~\\citep{singh2023restem,zelikman2022star} and only use the labeled training data.\n\n% then we will study the impact of the unlabeled training data in \\textsection\\ref{sec:unlabeled}.\n% More details can be referred to Appendix~\\ref{app:hyperparam}.\n% \\jh{if we do not validate for mathvista and always use the same checkpoint for both mathv360k and mathvista, it is worth emphasizing that here which could make our ood results more impressive}\n% To balance effectiveness with training efficiency, \n% We set the maximum number of training steps as 10K for all experiments. \n% \\jh{I think we should mention some key hyperparams here, like k, and temperature?}For additional details, please refer to Appendix \\ref{app:hyperparam}. \n% \\wl{Should we mention constant scheduler here? Or we mention it on other 3.2?}\n\n\n% \\jh{Note: this is a separate paragraph not belonging to training and evaluation, it should be at the end of 3.1}\n% {For ease of space,} we mainly \\jhc{use}{report the} MiniCPM-V-2.5 \\jhc{}{results} in \\jhc{analyzing and finalizing different design choices.}{the following analysis with full details, while we include the results of Phi-3.5-Vision and InternVL2-2B later in \\S \\ref{sec:final_recipe}.} \n% However, we also include more models, Phi-3.5-Vision and InternVL2-2B to demonstrate the effectiveness of our final recipe in \\S \\ref{sec:final_recipe}. \n\n\\subsection{Training Methods}\n\\label{sec:training_methods}\n% We start from a unified form \\citep{shao2024deepseekmath} for the learning algorithm $\\mathcal{A}$ of self-evolving training.\n% \\begin{equation}\n% \\nabla_{\\theta} \\mathcal{J}_{\\mathcal{A}}(\\theta) = \\mathbb{E}_{(q,o) \\sim \\mathcal{D}} \\left[ \\frac{1}{|o|} \\sum_{t=1}^{|o|} \\textcolor{red}{GC_{\\mathcal{A}}(q, o, t, \\pi_{rf})} \\nabla_{\\theta} \\log \\pi_{\\theta}(o_t | q, o_{<t}) \\right].\n% \\end{equation}\nAs described in \\textsection\\ref{sec:overview}, there are multiple variants on how we would train to update the policy model. \n% \\jhc{We decouple the variation dimensions by thinking of the gap between iterative training and online RL -- when the iteration interval is small, the checkpoint at each iteration is initialized from one from the last iteration, and the optimizer as well as the learning rate scheduler is inherited between iterations, then iterative training becomes an online RL algorithm. Therefore, we cluster the variations by three factors:}\nPrevious works mainly vary the model initialization factor, where at the ``Improve'' step,\n% \\jhc{(1) {\\bf Model initialization}: when training is performed at the ``Improve'' step,}{} \nthe model can be initialized from either the last checkpoint~\\citep{xu2024chatglm,pang2024iterative} or the beginning checkpoint before the first iteration~\\citep{zelikman2022star,singh2023restem}.\n% \\jhc{;}{\nBesides model initialization, in this work, we introduce new variants of iterative self-evolving through delving into the gap between iterative training and online RL -- concretely, when the iteration interval is small, the checkpoint at each iteration is initialized from one from the last iteration, and the optimizer as well as the learning rate scheduler is inherited between iterations, then iterative training becomes an online RL algorithm. \nTherefore, we propose {\\bf Continuous Self-Evolving}, a new iterative self-evolving training variant that represents a smoother interpolation between iterative training and online training. \nIn continuous self-evolving training, we inherit the optimizers as well as the learning rate schedulers from the last iteration besides inheriting the model checkpoint, so that the optimization is continuous and closer to purely online learning algorithms.   \n% }\n% \\jhc{\n% (2) {\\bf Iteration Interval}: while the common practice is to adopt a long iteration interval to process all the data queries for one iteration, we study the effect of having a shorter iteration interval, bringing it closer to online learning; and\n% (3) {\\bf Continuous Optimization}: we propose to inherit the optimizers as well as the learning rate schedulers from the last iteration besides inheriting the model checkpoint, so that the optimization is continuous and closer to purely online learning algorithms. }{} \nThis way, we only have a global optimizer and learning rate scheduler essentially across the entire iterative training process.\n% \\jhc{}{\nWe also analyze the \\emph{iteration interval} effect in continuous self-evolving, which is defined as the training queries passed for one iteration -- we specifically study the effect of having a shorter iteration interval, which stands in contrast to the common practice that adopts a long iteration interval to process all the data queries for one iteration.\n% }\n% \\jhc{We note that while the variation on model initialization has been studied before, the other two factors, iteration interval and continuous optimization, have been rarely discussed in previous implementations of iterative self-evolving training, and they turn out to be important empirically as we will show next. }{}\n% \\fan{small advice: maybe mention show in which section.}\n\n\n% the training method determines how data is processed and utilized to update the current policy model $\\pi^t_\\theta$ during self-evolving training. Unlike traditional supervised fine-tuning, which relies on static, \\textbf{offline} data curated by humans or models like GPT-4 \\citep{openai2023gpt4v}, self-evolving training emphasizes dynamic, \\textbf{online} data generation and exploitation. This online nature operates on two critical dimensions: data and learning. On the data dimension, the model samples from its own evolving policy, ensuring alignment with the current policy's behavior. On the learning side, the model continually trains from its current state‚Äîpreserving ongoing training dynamics such as optimizer states‚Äîwithout resetting or reinitializing, mitigating distributional shifts and maintaining coherence with the evolving policy.\n\n% In this section, we explore the impact of these two dimensions‚Äîdata sampling and learning dynamics‚Äîon the performance of self-evolving training. We begin by contrasting two paradigms: \\textbf{iterative} and \\textbf{online} learning. The iterative approach decouples data sampling from training, where the model samples data and then trains on it, either starting from an SFT model $\\pi_{\\theta_{sft}}$ or the current policy, without carrying over optimizer states or learning schedules. In contrast, the online paradigm integrates data sampling and training into a continuous process, maintaining the model's training state and ensuring ongoing alignment between learning and the evolving data distribution.\n\n\n\\textbf{Setup}\n% \\jh{across the paper, sometimes we have ``:'' after the paragraph title, sometimes we don't, please be consistent}\nWe perform controlled experiments to study the effect of different training methods, thus in this experiment we use the labeled dataset only and simply adopt the binary exact-match reward between ground-truth answer $a^*$ and the generated answer. \nWe compare with the most common iterative self-evolving algorithms ReST$^{EM}$~\\citep{singh2023restem} and iterative RFT, which are specific instantiations of our training methods design space. \n% \\jhc{To distinguish from the baselines, the variants with continuous optimization are named as \\emph{Continuous Self-Evolving}.}\n% {} \nTo study the effect of iteration interval in the proposed continuous self-evolving, we experiment with different percentage of all the queries per iteration, varying from [6.25\\%, 12.5\\%, 25\\%, 50\\%, 100\\%].\n% For iterative learning, we examine methods like ReST-EM \\citep{singh2023restem} and iterative RFT. In ReST-EM, training restarts from the SFT model at each iteration, while iterative RFT begins from the current policy model but reinitializes training dynamics at each iteration. On the other hand, the online paradigm, represented by online RFT, provides more efficient and scalable learning by continuously updating the model without resetting training states, making it particularly suitable for multimodal reasoning tasks.\n% From a data perspective, we analyze how varying the degree of online data sampling affects performance.\n% Specifically, we experiment with different rollout batch sizes for online RFT‚Äî[6.25\\%, 12.5\\%, 50\\%, 100\\%] of the dataset‚Äîto assess how the degree of online sampling influences the effectiveness of training in this evolving context.\n\n\\begin{table}[t!]\n  \\centering\n  \\caption{\n  % \\jh{the full results of mathvista will be useful on this table -- if we can still find it, plz record it. For here, it is better to have the table with full mathvista results in appendix} \\jl{we already have them in Table \\ref{tab:full_mathvista}, I think adding one hyperlink is enough} \n  Accuracy results (\\%) of self-evolving training using various training methods and iteration intervals.\n  % \\jh{explain what the two numbers in iteration interval column mean}\n  % \\jh{not consistent, we call it iteration interval in all other places even in the first sentence of this caption. Iteration interval means interval between iterations which I think is better}\n  Interval (\\#) stands for iteration interval, the ratio of data we traverse in one iteration, and we also record the number of corresponding queries.\n  $\\mathcal{M}$ represents the policy model from which training is initialized in each iteration. $\\mathcal{O}$ denotes whether the optimization process is continuous, i.e., the optimizer states and lr scheduler are inherited from the last checkpoint. Please refer to Table \\ref{tab:full_mathvista} to check the full results on all sub-tasks of MathVista.\n  }\n  % \\jh{briefly explain in the caption what it means when it is continuous or not}\n  % \\jh{why did we remove the SFT baselines in the table? I think we should have them in the first table to show our RFT stuff is successful and at least improves baselines}}\n\\setlength{\\tabcolsep}{2pt}\n  \\resizebox{0.48\\textwidth}{!}{\n  \\begin{tabular}{lllrrr}\n    \\toprule\n    Method   & $\\mathcal{M}$  & $\\mathcal{O}$ &  Interval (\\%) & MathV360K & MathVista \\\\\n    \\midrule\n     MiniCPM-V-2.5 & - & -&- & 13.6 & 52.4 \\\\\n     \\ \\ \\ \\ +warmup &- &- &- & 38.8 & 52.6 \\\\\n     SFT& - & -&- & 44.3 & 54.8 \\\\\n     \\midrule\n     Iterative RFT   & $\\pi_{\\theta}^t$  &  $\\times$  & 100\\% & 42.3 & 55.7 \\\\\n     Rest$^{EM}$ & $\\pi_{\\theta}^0$ & $\\times$   & 100\\% & 42.3 & 55.1 \\\\\n    \\midrule\n     \\multirow{5}{*}{\\makecell{Continous\\\\ Self-Evolving}} \n     & \\multirow{5}{*}{$\\pi_{\\theta}^t$} \n     & \\multirow{5}{*}{$\\checkmark$} \n     & 100\\% & 42.2 & 56.7 \\\\\n     & & & 50\\% & \\textbf{43.1} & 56.2  \\\\\n     & & & 25\\% & \\textbf{43.1} & \\textbf{57.2}  \\\\\n     & & & 12.5\\% & 42.3 & 56.1  \\\\\n     & & & 6.25\\% & 41.0 & 56.8  \\\\\n    \\bottomrule\n\\end{tabular}}\n  \\label{tab:online_rft}%\n    \\vspace{-10pt}\n\\end{table}\n\n\\textbf{Results}\n% \\jh{We don't need to make miniCPM special since we have the full results of other two models. Here when cite the results, plz cite Table 5 as well to draw conclusions based on results of 3 models, not just one}\nTable \\ref{tab:online_rft} presents the experimental results of various training methods. Overall, initializing training from the last policy model checkpoint $\\pi_{\\theta}^t$ and maintaining a continuous optimization process contribute most significantly to the effectiveness of self-evolving training, particularly on MathVista. Continuous self-evolving achieves the best performance both on the in-domain MathV360K test set, with 43.1\\%, and on the OOD test set, MathVista, with 57.2\\%. We also see the importance of maintaining a proper interval to traverse the data queries. With a large interval, the training method becomes closer to an offline one, and the model cannot get timely updates on data matching its current output distribution. On the other hand, switching over the \\textit{Improve} and \\textit{Generate} steps too frequently makes the learning process unstable, leading to a lower score, especially on the in-domain test set. The strategy of continuous self-evolving with proper intervals also works for other smaller models, as shown in Table \\ref{tab:full_mathvista} compared with representative baselines, indicating its effectiveness and generalizability across different model sizes.\n\n% \\jl{Hope we can also add avg rollout utils here to indicate 45k is good for exploitation}\n\n% \\begin{table}[t!]\n%   \\centering\n%   \\caption{Experiments for Section 3.1 - Training Algorithm\\jh{Are the RestEM results from the same training steps or longer?}\\jh{maybe we should report on both benchmarks}\\jh{we should add the baseline results to the table (the initial performance before rft)}}\n%     \\begin{tabular}{llllrrrrr}\n%     \\toprule\n%     status &   Method   & ckpt  & optimizer & data per interval & perf & perf id & perf avg & Roll utils \\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%     finished (mbzui)  & IRT   & last  & new   & 100\\% (180K) & 55.7 & 42.6 & xx.x & \\\\\n%     finished (mbzui)  & RestEM & first & new   & 100\\% (180K) & xx.xx & xx.xx & xx.xx \\\\\n%     idle  & IRT global eval   & last  & new   & 100\\% (180K) & & & & \\\\\n%     idle  & RestEM global eval & first & new   & 100\\% (180K) & & & & \\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%     finished (mbzui) & \\multirow{5}[2]{*}{online RFT} & \\multirow{5}[2]{*}{last} & \\multirow{5}[2]{*}{last} & 100\\% (180K) & 56.7 & 42.2 & 49.45 & \\\\\n%     finished (mbzui)  &       &       &       & 50\\% (90K) & 56.2 & 43.1 & 49.65 &\\\\\n%     finished (mbzui) &       &       &       & 25\\%  (45K) & 57.2 & 43.1 & 50.15 & \\\\\n%     finished (mbzui) &       &       &       & 12.5\\% (22K) & 56.1 & 42.3 & 49.2 &\\\\\n%     finished (mbzui) &       &       &       & 6.25\\% (11K) & 56.8 & 41.0 & 48.9 &\\\\\n%     \\bottomrule\n%     \\end{tabular}%\n%   \\label{tab:addlabel}%\n% \\end{table}%\n\n% For online RFT, we experiment with different sampling intervals, using [6.25\\%, 12.5\\%, 25\\%, 50\\%, 100\\%] of the dataset in each iteration.\n\n% From the perspective of data dimension, we investigate how varying online degrees of data sampling in different \n\n% \\begin{table}[h!]\n%     \\centering\n%     \\begin{tabular}{llll|}\n%         \\hline\n%         \\textbf{Methods} & \\textbf{Data Source} & \\textbf{Reward Function} & \\textbf{Gradient Coefficient} \\\\\n%         \\hline\n%         SFT & $q, o \\sim P_{sft}(Q, O)$ & - (11K) & 56.8 & 44.1 & 50.45 &\\\\\n%     \\bottomrule\n%     \\end{tabular}%\n%   \\label{tab:addlabel}%\n% \\end{table}%\n\n% With decay.\n\n% \\paragraph{Other Settings}\n% \\begin{itemize}\n%     \\item K (number of rollout per query) = 16\n%     \\item Temperature = 1.0\n%     \\item Max. global steps = 10K\n% \\end{itemize}\n\n% \\begin{table}[h]\n%     \\centering\n%     \\begin{tabular}{c|c|c}\n%     \\toprule\n%         Name & State & Priority \\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%     \\multicolumn{3}{c}{Iterative Rejection Fine Tuning (IRFT)} \\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%         IRFT from last ckpt but new optimizer, only new data & TO RUN & P0 \\\\\n%         IRFT from initial ckpt but new data mixed with old data & TO RUN & Optional \\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%     \\multicolumn{3}{c}{Rest-EM} \\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%         IRT from initial ckpt and new optimizer (Sampling from the 180K per iteration) & TO RUN & P0\\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%     \\multicolumn{3}{c}{Online RFT} \\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%         with 180K & TO RUN & P0\\\\\n%         with 90K & TO RUN & P0\\\\\n%         with 45K & TO RUN & P0\\\\\n%         with 22.5K & TO RUN & P0\\\\\n%         with 11.25K & TO RUN & P0\\\\\n%     \\bottomrule\n%     % \\midrule\n%     % \\multicolumn{3}{c}{Reference Baseline} \\\\\n%     % \\midrule\n%     %      &  \\\\\n%     %      &  \\\\\n%     % \\bottomrule\n%     \\end{tabular}\n%     \\caption{TODO}\n%     \\label{tab:my_label}\n% \\end{table}\n\n% After this section, we fix the training algorithm and the interval.\n\n\\subsection{Reward Models}\n\\label{sec:rm}\nIn self-evolving training, the most common approach to reward function design uses a binary reward $\\mathcal{R}(\\hat{y}_i) = \\mathds{1}(\\hat{a}_i = a^*)$, where $\\hat{a}_i$ is the predicted answer inside $\\hat{y}_i$ and incorrect responses are filtered out to maximize rewards. While effective, this sparse binary reward has limitations. It overlooks the quality of the intermediate reasoning steps within a response. Additionally, reward models trained from equal or higher capacity models than the policy model \\citep{fried2022incoder,wang2024mathshepherd,sun2024easy2hard} can provide richer signals to improve the policy model‚Äôs learning. \n\nIn this section, we introduce a Process Reward Model (PRM) \\citep{lightman2023let, wang2024mathshepherd}\n% \\jh{cite some PRM papers} \nfor multimodal reasoning‚Äîthe first of its kind, to our knowledge‚Äîand explore how integrating PRM can enhance reward design and whether it can improve policy model learning in self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning. To incorporate the reward scores into the objective of self-evolving training, the reward function is reformulated as:\n% \\fan{equation shoule be numbered.}\n\\begin{align}\n&\\mathcal{R}(\\hat{y}_i) = \\mathcal{H}(\\mathds{1}(a^* = \\hat{a}_i)\\times \\mathcal{R}_p(\\hat{y}_i))\\\\\n&\\mathcal{R}_p(\\hat{y}_i) = \\min(f(s_{i}^0), f(s_{i}^1),..., f(s_{i}^m))\n\\end{align}\nHere, $\\mathcal{H}$ is an operation that processes responses based on the final reward scores, where we ensure all responses are correct by matching the ground truths,\n% \\jh{we need to explain here this definition means we have pre-filtering and all the responses must be correct on the labeled data.}\n and $\\mathcal{R}_p(\\hat{y}_i)$ represents the process reward score for each sampled response. The function $f(s_i^k)$ denotes the reward score at each intermediate step. Following \\citet{lightman2023let}, we use the $\\min$ operation to aggregate stepwise rewards.\n% ensuring that the overall reward reflects the lowest-scoring step in the reasoning process.\n\n% \\paragraph{Training Reward Models:}\n% \\jh{Please just use two or three sentences to describe how we train the reward models and move many details in this paragraph to appendix (even in appendix we don't need to describe math-shepherd in detail). We can just say we follow math-shepherd xxxx [two sentences on the high-level description of the approach].\n% We are not inventing this PRM training approach, thus we don't need to write this long to describe it. \n% If this is just two or three sentences, it could be merged into Setup paragraph.\n% }\n\n% To train our PRM, we first train another checkpoint \n% (denoted as $\\hat{\\pi}_{\\theta}^0$) on our CoT-augmented training data for a much longer period to make sure it fully converges. \n\n% Based on this model, we leverage Monte Carlo Rollut method~\\citep{wang2024mathshepherd} to collect the training data for PRM. Specially, we randomly pick 50K questions from the full training set, and sample 16 responses for each of them with $\\hat{\\pi}_{\\theta}^0$. We de-duplicate these responses, and only keep at most 4 responses for each question. After that we randomly sample 50K question-response pairs from all the pairs, where we control the ratio of correct and wrong responses as 1:1, and the ratio of multi-choice and free-form question as 1:1 as well, to keep a balanced distribution. \n\n% To construct the labels of each step, we use $\\hat{\\pi}_{\\theta}^0$ as the completer to complete the solution from the end of each step in one response. For the $k^{\\text{th}}$ step, the step label is annotated as $\\frac{1}{N}\\sum_{j=1}^{N}{\\mathds{1}(C_j(s^{\\leq k})=a^*)}$, where $N (=16)$ is the number of completion, $C_j$ is the $j$-th completion.\n\n% Based on the stepwise annotations, we train our PRM from $\\hat{\\pi}_{\\theta}^0$. We initialize the linear reward model head as the average of the embeddings, and train with MSE loss on all tokens, where the label of each token is identical to the step end token. In experiments we freeze the visual encoder as we find it brings a slight improvement. \n% \\wl{It include too many training details consider to move sth to appendix}\n\n\\textbf{Setup}\nWe conduct controlled experiments to assess the impact of incorporating the Process Reward Model (PRM) into self-evolving training and explore how best to utilize it. Notably, before applying PRM, responses are pre-filtered based on their final answers to ensure consistency and quality during training. To train our PRM, we use Monte Carlo rollouts starting from prefixes with partial reasoning steps \\citep{wang2024mathshepherd} to generate the training data. Specifically, we sample 16 responses per question and complete each step 8 times to obtain step-level annotations and more details can be found in Appendix \\ref{app:prm}. We evaluate two different $\\mathcal{H}$ operations: (1) Top-K: Pick the top-K correct responses according to their reward scores, and (2) Filtering by a Threshold $\\alpha$: Filtering out sampled responses with lower aggregated rewards than $\\alpha$. The optimal value of $\\alpha$ is set $0.2$ by grid searching on the validation set. Additionally, we investigate how varying the value of $\\operatorname{K}$ in $\\operatorname{Top-K}$ affects training, as it represents a trade-off between the quality and diversity of the samples. According to \\textsection\\ref{sec:training_methods}, we fix training methods as continuous self-evolving with 45k interval and set continuous self-evolving, with or without randomly selected correct responses as our baselines.\n\n\\textbf{Results}\n% See Table \\ref{tab:sec33_main}\n% \\jh{We don't need to make miniCPM special since we have the full results of other two models. Here when cite the results, plz cite Table 5 as well to draw conclusions based on results of 3 models, not just one}\nTable \\ref{tab:sec33_main} presents the results of integrating the PRM into self-evolving training, along with the impact of different $\\mathcal{H}$ choices. Continuous Self-Evolving with PRM using $\\operatorname{Top-2}$ achieves the best performance in both the ID and OOD tests, with scores of 45.3\\% and 59.2\\%, respectively. Compared to training without PRM, most instances of self-evolving training with PRM show improved performance, especially in the OOD test. Interestingly, randomly selecting a subset of correct responses actually leads to worse performance than continuous self-evolving, suggesting that even correct answers can be noisy. Random selection may increase the proportion of these noisy samples, undermining the effectiveness of self-evolving training.\n\n% In terms of leveraging PRM, we found that using $\\operatorname{Top-K}$ with a moderate number of responses‚Äîa re-ranking operation to select $\\operatorname{K}$ correct responses with the highest-quality intermediate steps‚Äîoutperforms filtering by a threshold.\nIn terms of leveraging PRM, we found that using $\\operatorname{Top-K}$ to select the a fixed number of best $\\operatorname{K}$ responses with high-quality intermediate steps outperforms threshold-based filtering.\nThe results also highlight the importance of balancing the quality and diversity of sampled responses. Selecting $\\operatorname{K}=2$ strikes this balance well, ensuring both response diversity and high-quality reasoning steps for each question. Similar to the results in \\S \\ref{sec:training_methods}, we also see improvement when involving PRM on smaller models in Table \\ref{tab:full_mathvista}, Appendix \\ref{ap:full_mathvista_analysis}.\n\n\\begin{table}[!t]\n  \\centering\n  \\caption{The results of self-evolving training with PRM and different strategies to leverage reward scores. $\\mathcal{H}$ is the method to further pick out high-quality responses from the correct rollouts: (1) $\\operatorname{Top-k}$ is to select K correct responses with highest rewards, and (2) $>\\alpha$ is to pick out the correct responses with rewards larger than $\\alpha$.\n  % \\jh{what is Standard? Plz always try to make the table/figures self-contained, like readers can read the table without reading the text in the sections. if Standard is Continuous Self-Evolving, just write Continuous Self-Evolving} \n  % \\jh{we don't call it online RFT}\n  % \\wl{Consider to draw a figure if we have time to do that}\n  % \\fan{first column is redundant?}\n  Please refer to Table \\ref{tab:full_mathvista} to check the full results on all sub-tasks of MathVista.\n  } \n  \\setlength{\\tabcolsep}{2.2pt}\n    \\resizebox{0.48\\textwidth}{!}{\n    \\begin{tabular}{lllrrrr}\n    \\toprule\n    Method & $\\mathcal{H}$ & PRM & MathV360K & MathVista \\\\\n    \\midrule\n    \\makecell{Cont.\\\\ Self-Evolving} & - & $\\times$ &  43.1 & 57.2 \\\\\n    \\ \\ \\ \\ + Random & $\\operatorname{Random-2}$  & $\\times$ &  41.0 & 55.5 \\\\\n    \\midrule\n    \\multirow{4}[0]{*}{\\ \\ \\ \\ \\makecell{+PRM-based\\\\ Selection}}  & $> \\alpha$  & \\multirow{4}[0]{*}{$\\checkmark$}& 43.8 & 57.5 \\\\ \n     & $\\operatorname{Top-1}$ &  & 43.0 & 59.0  \\\\\n     &$\\operatorname{Top-2}$ &  & \\textbf{45.3} & \\textbf{59.2} \\\\\n     &$\\operatorname{Top-4}$ &  & 44.0 & 58.4 \\\\\n    \\bottomrule\n    \\end{tabular}%\n    }\n  \\label{tab:sec33_main}%\n    \\vspace{-10pt}\n\\end{table}%\n\n\\textbf{What makes PRM work for self-evolving training?}\nTo pursue deeper insights into the role of PRM in self-evolving training, we conduct an analysis presented in Figure~\\ref{fig:sec33rm_quality}. Based on the results from \\textsection\\ref{sec:rm}, we explore PRM‚Äôs impact from two key perspectives: (1) Can PRM help the model to select out correct responses among multiple rollouts? (2) How different are the Top 2 and the rest correct solutions re-ranked by reward scores? We use the first checkpoint after warmup $\\pi_\\theta^0$ as policy model to sample 16 responses for each question in the validation set with temperature=$1.0$ and reveal the behaviors of PRM in these samples. \n\nWe evaluate the verification ability of our PRM using two metrics, Best-of-N (BoN) and weighted voting \\citep{sun2024easy2hard}, which are commonly employed to assess the performance of reward models. Surprisingly, as shown in Figure~\\ref{fig:sec33rm_1}, our PRM underperforms in both metrics. Notably, BoN and weighted voting yield worse results than vanilla majority voting when $N<16$. We speculate that this is due to the lack of high-quality step-level annotations compared to text-only reasoning tasks. These findings suggest that our PRM is not an effective \\textbf{verifier}.\n\nTo understand why our PRM can still significantly contributes to self-evolving training despite its weaker verification abilities, we analyzed the distribution of other metrics for the top-2 selected responses compared to other correct responses. We approached this from two perspectives: the average number of reasoning steps, and how much a response is directly relevant to the question (see Appendix \\ref{app:gpt4orela}), since we do not find incorrect steps but find some irrelevant steps after randomly checking some examples.\nWe found the responses re-ranked by our PRM generally have \\textbf{fewer reasoning steps} (Figure \\ref{fig:rm_steps} in Appendix~\\ref{ap:dist_steps}) and \\textbf{more relevant to the query} (Figure~\\ref{fig:sec33rm_3}). This highlights the \\textbf{precision} of our PRM in recognizing genuinely high-quality responses. \n% While it may not rank all correct responses, it consistently selects those that are truly high quality, avoiding false positives. \nTherefore, our PRM acts as an effective \\textbf{reranker} to identify top-quality responses. This is especially critical when responses are already filtered by ground-truth answers, and the ability to accurately assess the quality of reasoning steps beyond accuracy becomes vital.\n\n\\begin{figure}[!t]  % Âä†ÂÖ•[htbp]Ôºå‰ΩøÂõæÂÉèÊõ¥ÂÆπÊòìÊéíÂ∏É\n    \\centering\n    \\begin{subfigure}[b]{0.23\\textwidth}\n        \\centering\n        \\includegraphics[width=1\\linewidth]\n        {figures/sec3.3/MATHV360KEVAL250_temperature-1.0_acc.pdf}\n        \\caption{}\n        \\label{fig:sec33rm_1}\n    \\end{subfigure}\n    % \\begin{subfigure}[b]{0.3\\textwidth}  % ËÆæÁΩÆÊØè‰∏™Â≠êÂõæÁöÑÂÆΩÂ∫¶‰∏∫0.32\\textwidth\n    %     \\centering\n    %     \\includegraphics[width=0.85\\linewidth]{figures/sec3.3/rm_steps.pdf}\n    %     \\caption{}\n    %     \\label{fig:sec33rm_2}\n    % \\end{subfigure}\n    \\begin{subfigure}[b]{0.23\\textwidth}  % ËÆæÁΩÆÊØè‰∏™Â≠êÂõæÁöÑÂÆΩÂ∫¶‰∏∫0.32\\textwidth\n        \\centering\n        \\includegraphics[width=1\\linewidth]{figures/sec3.3/rm_relscore.pdf}\n        \\caption{}\n        \\label{fig:sec33rm_3}\n    \\end{subfigure}\n    \\caption{\n        \\textbf{(a)}: Accuracy on the val. set of greedy decoding and three selection strategy across different numbers of rollouts;\n        \\textbf{(b)}: Average relativity score annotated by GPT4-o of Top 2 and the rest responses re-ranked by rewards, we only calculate on correct ones.\n        % For distribution of average \\# of steps, please refer to Appendix~\\ref{ap:dist_steps} \\wl{move steps figure to appendix. fixed content in text parts}\n    }\n    \\label{fig:sec33rm_quality}\n    \\vspace{-15pt}\n\\end{figure}\n\n% As shown in Figures~\\ref{fig:sec33rm_2} and \\ref{fig:sec33rm_3},\n\n% \\jh{these are cool results! I wonder why we are thinking of scoring relativity rather than correctness like using gpt4-o as PRM (reviewers may wonder too)}\n\nIn addition to the aforementioned analysis, we also investigate why leveraging $\\alpha$ to filter responses with lower reward scores performs worse than $\\operatorname{Top-K}$. The results indicate that, even with the optimal threshold value determined from the validation set, it tends to either retain or filter out all responses for each query, which reduces diversity and makes the learning process more challenging. This further supports the conclusion that \\textbf{our PRM performs better as a Reranker than as a Verifier}.\n\n% we hypothesize ... According to ..., we find...  here for self-evolving training for multimodal reasoning.\n\n% In Figure \\ref{fig:sec33rm_quality} we find that our RM is unsuitable to be a verifier, but if we have already known several answers for a questions are correct, we can use the RM as a re-ranker to distinguish them.  \n\n% On the other hand, setting a threshold $\\theta$ is bad, since in most cases (130/181), the $\\theta$ we pick as the optimal one one on the dev set can not distinguish responses that are correct.\n\n% The goal of analysis in this section is to provide basic intuition why using RM is better than not, and why reranking is better than theta based.\n\n\n\n% Table generated by Excel2LaTeX from sheet 'Sheet1'\n% \\begin{table}\n%   \\centering\n%   \\caption{Experiments for Section 3.2 - Why and How to use RM, $\\theta^\\star$ is selected on the validation set: $\\theta^\\star=\\argmax_{\\theta} f_1(\\theta, M|D)$}\n%     \\begin{tabular}{llrrrr}\n%     \\toprule\n%     status & selection & \\multicolumn{1}{l}{num per query} & interval & perf & perf id\\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%     idle (from 3.1) & - & - & 45k & 57.2 & 43.1 \\\\\n%     finished (xiwen)  & random & 2 & & 55.5 & 41.0 \\\\\n%     running (mbzui)  & \\multirow{4}[0]{*}{rerank} & 1 & & 59.0 & 43.0  \\\\\n%     running (mbzui xiwen)  &       & 2 & $45k$ & 59.2 & 45.3 \\\\\n%     running (mbzui)  &       & 4 & & 58.4 & 44.0 \\\\\n%     idle  & threshold & $>\\theta^\\star$ & & 57.5 & 43.8\\\\\n%     \\bottomrule\n%     \\end{tabular}%\n%   \\label{tab:sec33_main}%\n% \\end{table}%\n% \\begin{table}\n%   \\centering\n%   \\caption{Experiments for Section 3.2 - Why and How to use RM, $\\theta^\\star$ is selected on the validation set: $\\theta^\\star=\\argmax_{\\theta} f_1(\\theta, M|D)$}\n%     \\begin{tabular}{llrrrr}\n%     \\toprule\n%     status & selection & \\multicolumn{1}{l}{num per query} & interval & perf & perf id\\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%     idle (from 3.1) & - & - & 45k & 57.2 & 43.4 \\\\\n%     finished (xiwen)  & random & 2 & & 55.5 & 41.0 \\\\\n%     running (mbzui)  & \\multirow{4}[0]{*}{rerank} & 1 & & 59.0 & 43.0  \\\\\n%     running (mbzui xiwen)  &       & 2 & $\\boldsymbol{45k}$ & 59.2 & 46.0 \\\\\n%     running (mbzui)  &       & 4 & & 58.4 & 45.9 \\\\\n%     idle  & threshold & $>\\theta^\\star$ & & 57.5 & 43.8\\\\\n%     \\bottomrule\n%     \\end{tabular}%\n%   \\label{tab:addlabel}%\n% \\end{table}%\n\n\n% \\begin{table}[htbp]\n%   \\centering\n%   \\caption{Experiments for Section 3.2}\n%     \\begin{tabular}{llllr}\n%     \\toprule\n%     status &       & ckpt  & optimizer & data per interval \\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%     idle  & IRT   & last  & new   & 100\\% (180K) \\\\\n%     idle  & IRT-plus & last  & last  & 100\\%+ (180K+) \\\\\n%     idle  & RestEM & first & new   & 100\\% (180K) \\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%     idle  & \\multirow{5}[2]{*}{online RFT} & \\multirow{5}[2]{*}{last} & \\multirow{5}[2]{*}{last} & 100\\% (180K) \\\\\n%     idle  &       &       &       & 50\\% (90K) \\\\\n%     idle  &       &       &       & 25\\%  (45K) \\\\\n%     idle  &       &       &       & 12.5\\% (22K) \\\\\n%     idle  &       &       &       & 6.25\\% (11K) \\\\\n%     \\bottomrule\n%     \\end{tabular}%\n%   \\label{tab:addlabel}%\n% \\end{table}%\n\n\n\n\n\n\\subsection{Prompt Variation}\n\\label{sec:unlabeled}\n% \\jh{as we discussed on slack, we study threshold and when to add the prompts?}\nIn this section, we explore how prompt variation affects self-evolving training. There are two primary types of prompts: labeled prompts and unlabeled prompts. Labeled prompts come with annotated ground truth answers, which can be used to filter out incorrect responses during training. In contrast, utilizing unlabeled prompts in self-evolving training is more challenging due to the absence of ground truth annotations. To maintain the quality of unlabeled prompts in training, surrogates like reward scores or pseudo labels must be employed. Meanwhile, unlike labeled prompts, unlabeled prompts are not be trained in SFT period, which increases the difficulty of learning for policy models. \n\n\\textbf{Skylines: Unlabeled Prompts with Oracle Reward Signals} The coupling of these additional factors introduces complexity, making the effective use of unlabeled prompts less predictable. Therefore we start by establishing a baseline with ``skyline\" experiments, where both the unlabeled prompts and their ground truth answers are available but not used during the SFT phase. These unlabeled prompts with oracle reward signals serve as an intermediate difficulty between fully unlabeled and labeled prompts, providing insight into the challenges of training with unlabeled data.\n\n\\textbf{Unlabeled Prompts} We incorporate unlabeled prompts into self-evolving training. To ensure the quality of sampled responses for these prompts, we use weighted voting to ensemble the predictions from different responses, treating the ensembled prediction as a pseudo label $\\Tilde{a}$. This pseudo label is then used to filter out responses with conflicting predictions, ensuring consistency. Following the best practices outlined in \\textsection\\ref{sec:rm}, we apply PRM as a reranker to select the top-2 responses with the predicted answer $\\Tilde{a}$. These unlabeled prompts are then mixed with labeled ones for self-evolving training. Additionally, since learning from unlabeled prompts is more challenging for policy models, we investigate the optimal timing to introduce them into training as well.\n% to better understand their impact on model performance. \nWe maintain an interval of 45k prompts and adjust when unlabeled prompts are introduced into the training process. Specifically, we introduce unlabeled prompts after [0\\%, 25\\%, 50\\%, 75\\%] of the total training process.\n% \\wl{Its clarity could be improved}\n\n\n\n\n\\textbf{A Glimpse at Unlabeled Prompts: Potential Efforts to Make Them Effective} Table~\\ref{tab:unlabeled} presents the results of incorporating unlabeled prompts with and without oracle reward signals.\nWhen training relies solely on oracle reward signals without the PRM, continuous self-evolving with unlabeled prompts outperforms standard continuous self-evolving trained only on labeled prompts in the out-of-domain test but underperforms in the in-domain test. \nThis indicates that additional prompts help the model generalize better to underrepresented questions but also increase the risk of forgetting previously learned information. \nHowever, after combining with our PRM, all policy models perform worse than our best model trained exclusively on labeled prompts in both benchmarks, even when oracle reward signals are provided. \nBased on the analysis in \\textsection\\ref{sec:rm}, this occurs since our PRM is unable to verify responses without ground-truth answers, and its generalization remains a concern.\n% \\begin{wraptable}[16]{r}{6.0cm}\n\n% \\end{wraptable}%\n\n\\begin{table}[]\n  \\centering\n  % \\small\n  % \\vspace{-.5mm}\n  % \\setlength{\\tabcolsep}{8pt}\n  \\caption{Results of involving unlabeled data. $T_{\\texttt{mixin}}$ denotes when to mixin the unlabeled data. The use of PRM follows \\S \\ref{sec:rm}, except we first get a pesudo ``ground truth\" through weighted voting on unlabeled prompts. \n  % \\fan{consider to shorten caption}\n  % \\wl{Just reformat it for now we can change it to a table at right side}\n  }\n  % \\vspace{-2.3mm}\n    \\resizebox{0.45\\textwidth}{!}{\n    \\begin{tabular}{llrrr}\n    \\toprule\n    Oracle & PRM   & $T_{\\texttt{mixin}}$ & MathV360K & MathVista\\\\\n    \\midrule\n    -     & $\\times$     & - & 43.1 & 57.2 \\\\\n    -     & $\\checkmark$     & - & 45.3 & 59.2 \\\\\n    $\\checkmark$     & $\\times$     & 0\\%   & 42.5 & 58.2 \\\\\n    $\\checkmark$     & $\\checkmark$     & 0\\%   & 42.9 & 59.1 \\\\\n    \\midrule\n    $\\times$     & $\\checkmark$     & 0\\%   & $43.3$ & $58.2$ \\\\\n    $\\times$     & $\\checkmark$     & 25\\% & 42.4 & 57.6 \\\\\n    $\\times$     & $\\checkmark$     & 50\\%  & 42.9 & 58.2 \\\\\n    $\\times$     & $\\checkmark$     & 75\\% & 45.0  & 58.8 \\\\\n    \\bottomrule\n    \\end{tabular}\n    }\n    \\label{tab:unlabeled}%\n    % \\vspace{-20pt}\n\\end{table}%\n\n\\begin{figure}\n    \\centering % ÂõæÁâáÂ±Ö‰∏≠\n    \\includegraphics[width=1\\linewidth]{figures//sec4/progress_or_regress_icml.pdf} % ÊèíÂÖ•ÂõæÁâáÔºåÂÆΩÂ∫¶5cmÔºåÊõøÊç¢‰∏∫ÂÆûÈôÖÂõæÁâáË∑ØÂæÑ\n    \\caption{Opposite trend of Greedy Accuracy and Pass@K.} % ÂõæÁâáÊ†áÈ¢ò\n    \\label{fig:sec4dynamics_1}\n    \\vspace{-15pt}\n\\end{figure}\n\nWhen examining the timing for introducing unlabeled prompts, we find that adding them from the beginning helps mitigate the negative impact on model performance, compared to introducing them in midway. However, when unlabeled prompts are introduced later in the training process, they participate less in the overall training, leading to better results simply due to their limited involvement. This suggests that, without sufficient surrogate supervision (e.g., precise reward signals), introducing unlabeled prompts into self-evolving training can harm the process, potentially causing a deviation in the policy model's distribution.\n\n% skyline: we use unlabeled queries as labeled queries (w/ RM and w/o RM) (1:1, 2:1)\n\n% by default, we mix with an intuitive ratio in the beginning (optionally we can try add since the half)\n\n% We don't change the interval. \n\n% After this section, we have a temporary recipe for both w/ and w/o unlabeled data. \n\n% \\begin{itemize}\n%     \\item Other datasets: Oracle, PRM\n%     \\item Synthetic datasets\n% \\end{itemize}\n% Table generated by Excel2LaTeX from sheet 'Sheet1'\n% \\begin{wraptable}{t}{8cm}\n% \\begin{table}[t!]\n%   \\centering\n%   \\caption{Results on the involvement of unlabeled data. $T_{mixin}$ denotes the starting point to mixin the unlabeled data into the labeled data. The use PRM is identical to \\S \\ref{sec:rm}, except we first get a pesudo ``groundtruth\" answer through weighted voting on unlabeled prompts. \\wl{Just reformat it for now we can change it to a table at right side}}\n%     \\begin{tabular}{llrrr}\n%     \\toprule\n%     Oracle & PRM   & $T_{mixin}$ & MathVista & MathV360K\\\\\n%     \\midrule\n%     -
